<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Mitochondrial DNA</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
</div>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mitochondrial_dna_lg.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="894" data-file-width="800" height="335" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Mitochondrial_dna_lg.jpg/300px-Mitochondrial_dna_lg.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Mitochondrial_dna_lg.jpg/450px-Mitochondrial_dna_lg.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Mitochondrial_dna_lg.jpg/600px-Mitochondrial_dna_lg.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mitochondrial_dna_lg.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Mitochondrial DNA is the small circular <a href="/wiki/Chromosome" title="Chromosome">chromosome</a> found inside mitochondria. These <a href="/wiki/Organelle" title="Organelle">organelles</a> found in cells have often been called the powerhouse of the cell.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Siekevitz_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-Siekevitz-1">[1]</a></sup> The mitochondria, and thus mitochondrial DNA, are passed only from <a href="/wiki/Mother" title="Mother">mother</a> to <a href="/wiki/Offspring" title="Offspring">offspring</a> through the <a href="/wiki/Egg_cell" title="Egg cell">egg cell</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><span id="Map_of_the_human_mitochondrial_genome"></span></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_the_human_mitochondrial_genome.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="991" data-file-width="1040" height="286" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Map_of_the_human_mitochondrial_genome.svg/300px-Map_of_the_human_mitochondrial_genome.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Map_of_the_human_mitochondrial_genome.svg/450px-Map_of_the_human_mitochondrial_genome.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Map_of_the_human_mitochondrial_genome.svg/600px-Map_of_the_human_mitochondrial_genome.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_the_human_mitochondrial_genome.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_mitochondrial_DNA" title="Human mitochondrial DNA">Human mitochondrial DNA</a> with the 37 genes on their respective H- and L-strands.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Electron_microscopy_reveals_mitochondrial_DNA_in_discrete_foci.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1236" data-file-width="956" height="284" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Electron_microscopy_reveals_mitochondrial_DNA_in_discrete_foci.jpg/220px-Electron_microscopy_reveals_mitochondrial_DNA_in_discrete_foci.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Electron_microscopy_reveals_mitochondrial_DNA_in_discrete_foci.jpg/330px-Electron_microscopy_reveals_mitochondrial_DNA_in_discrete_foci.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Electron_microscopy_reveals_mitochondrial_DNA_in_discrete_foci.jpg/440px-Electron_microscopy_reveals_mitochondrial_DNA_in_discrete_foci.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Electron_microscopy_reveals_mitochondrial_DNA_in_discrete_foci.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Electron microscopy reveals mitochondrial DNA in discrete foci. Bars: 200 nm. (A) Cytoplasmic section after <a href="/wiki/Immunogold_labelling" title="Immunogold labelling">immunogold labelling</a> with anti-DNA; gold particles marking mtDNA are found near the mitochondrial membrane (black dots in upper right). (B) Whole mount view of cytoplasm after extraction with CSK buffer and immunogold labelling with anti-DNA; mtDNA (marked by gold particles) resists extraction. From Iborra et al., 2004.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid15157274_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid15157274-2">[2]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p><b>Mitochondrial DNA</b> (<b>mtDNA</b> or <b>mDNA</b>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> is the <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> located in <a href="/wiki/Mitochondrion" title="Mitochondrion">mitochondria</a>, cellular organelles within <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eukaryotic" title="Eukaryotic">eukaryotic</a> cells that convert chemical energy from food into a form that cells can use, <a href="/wiki/Adenosine_triphosphate" title="Adenosine triphosphate">adenosine triphosphate</a> (ATP). Mitochondrial DNA is only a small portion of the DNA in a eukaryotic cell; most of the DNA can be found in the <a href="/wiki/Cell_nucleus" title="Cell nucleus">cell nucleus</a> and, in plants and algae, also in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plastids" title="Plastids">plastids</a> such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chloroplasts" title="Chloroplasts">chloroplasts</a>.</p>
<p>In humans, the 16,569 base pairs of mitochondrial DNA encode for only 37 <a href="/wiki/Gene" title="Gene">genes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-4">[4]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_mitochondrial_DNA" title="Human mitochondrial DNA">Human mitochondrial DNA</a> was the first significant part of the <a href="/wiki/Human_genome" title="Human genome">human genome</a> to be sequenced. In most species, including humans, mtDNA is inherited solely from the mother.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p>Since animal mtDNA evolves faster than nuclear genetic markers,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> it represents a mainstay of <a href="/wiki/Phylogenetics" title="Phylogenetics">phylogenetics</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Evolutionary" title="Evolutionary">evolutionary</a> biology. It also permits an examination of the relatedness of populations, and so has become important in <a href="/wiki/Anthropology" title="Anthropology">anthropology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Biogeography" title="Biogeography">biogeography</a>.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Origin">Origin</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mitochondrial_DNA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Origin">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Nuclear and mitochondrial DNA are thought to be of separate <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolutionary</a> origin, with the mtDNA being derived from the circular genomes of the <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">bacteria</a> that were engulfed by the early ancestors of today's eukaryotic cells. This theory is called the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Endosymbiotic_theory" title="Endosymbiotic theory">endosymbiotic theory</a>. Each mitochondrion is estimated to contain 2–10 mtDNA copies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wiesner_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wiesner-9">[9]</a></sup> In the cells of extant organisms, the vast majority of the proteins present in the mitochondria (numbering approximately 1500 different types in <a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">mammals</a>) are coded for by <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_DNA" title="Nuclear DNA">nuclear DNA</a>, but the genes for some, if not most, of them are thought to have originally been of bacterial origin, having since been transferred to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eukaryotic" title="Eukaryotic">eukaryotic</a> nucleus during <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolution</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Johnston.2C_I._G._and_Williams.2C_B._P._2016_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Johnston.2C_I._G._and_Williams.2C_B._P._2016-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>The reasons why mitochondria have retained some genes are debated. The existence in some species of mitochondrion-derived organelles lacking a genome<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> suggests that complete gene loss is possible, and transferring mitochondrial genes to the nucleus has several advantages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> The difficulty of targeting remotely-produced hydrophobic protein products to the mitochondrion is one hypothesis for why some genes are retained in mtDNA;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/CoRR_hypothesis" title="CoRR hypothesis">colocalisation for redox regulation</a> is another, citing the desirability of localised control over mitochondrial machinery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> Recent analysis of a wide range of mtDNA genomes suggests that both these features may dictate mitochondrial gene retention.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Johnston.2C_I._G._and_Williams.2C_B._P._2016_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-Johnston.2C_I._G._and_Williams.2C_B._P._2016-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Mitochondrial_inheritance">Mitochondrial inheritance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mitochondrial_DNA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Mitochondrial inheritance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In most <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metazoans" title="Metazoans">multicellular organisms</a>, mtDNA is inherited from the mother (maternally inherited). Mechanisms for this include simple dilution (an egg contains on average 200,000 mtDNA molecules, whereas a healthy human <a href="/wiki/Sperm" title="Sperm">sperm</a> was reported to contain on average 5 molecules<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> ), degradation of sperm mtDNA in the male genital tract, in the fertilized egg, and, at least in a few organisms, failure of sperm mtDNA to enter the egg. Whatever the mechanism, this single parent (<a href="/wiki/Uniparental_inheritance" title="Uniparental inheritance">uniparental inheritance</a>) pattern of mtDNA inheritance is found in most animals, most plants and in fungi as well.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Female_inheritance">Female inheritance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mitochondrial_DNA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Female inheritance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Sexual_reproduction" title="Sexual reproduction">sexual reproduction</a>, mitochondria are normally inherited exclusively from the mother; the mitochondria in mammalian sperm are usually destroyed by the egg cell after fertilization. Also, most mitochondria are present at the base of the sperm's tail, which is used for propelling the sperm cells; sometimes the tail is lost during fertilization. In 1999 it was reported that paternal sperm mitochondria (containing mtDNA) are marked with <a href="/wiki/Ubiquitin" title="Ubiquitin">ubiquitin</a> to select them for later destruction inside the <a href="/wiki/Embryo" title="Embryo">embryo</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> Some <i><a href="/wiki/In_vitro" title="In vitro">in vitro</a></i> fertilization techniques, particularly injecting a sperm into an <a href="/wiki/Oocyte" title="Oocyte">oocyte</a>, may interfere with this.</p>
<p>The fact that mitochondrial DNA is maternally inherited enables <a href="/wiki/Genetic_genealogy" title="Genetic genealogy">genealogical</a> researchers to trace <a href="/wiki/Matrilineality" title="Matrilineality">maternal lineage</a> far back in time. (<a href="/wiki/Y_chromosome" title="Y chromosome">Y-chromosomal DNA</a>, paternally inherited, is used in an analogous way to determine the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Patrilineal" title="Patrilineal">patrilineal</a> history.) This is usually accomplished on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_mitochondrial_DNA" title="Human mitochondrial DNA">human mitochondrial DNA</a> by sequencing the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hypervariable_control_region" title="Hypervariable control region">hypervariable control regions</a> (HVR1 or HVR2), and sometimes the complete molecule of the mitochondrial DNA, as a <a href="/wiki/Genealogical_DNA_test" title="Genealogical DNA test">genealogical DNA test</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> HVR1, for example, consists of about 440 base pairs. These 440 base pairs are then compared to the control regions of other individuals (either specific people or subjects in a database) to determine maternal lineage. Most often, the comparison is made to the revised <a href="/wiki/Cambridge_Reference_Sequence" title="Cambridge Reference Sequence">Cambridge Reference Sequence</a>. Vilà <i>et al.</i> have published studies tracing the matrilineal descent of domestic dogs to wolves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vila_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Vila-19">[19]</a></sup> The concept of the <a href="/wiki/Mitochondrial_Eve" title="Mitochondrial Eve">Mitochondrial Eve</a> is based on the same type of analysis, attempting to discover the origin of <a href="/wiki/Human" title="Human">humanity</a> by tracking the lineage back in time.</p>
<p>mtDNA is highly conserved, and its relatively slow mutation rates (compared to other DNA regions such as <a href="/wiki/Microsatellite" title="Microsatellite">microsatellites</a>) make it useful for studying the evolutionary relationships—<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phylogeny" title="Phylogeny">phylogeny</a>—of organisms. Biologists can determine and then compare mtDNA sequences among different species and use the comparisons to build an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Evolutionary_tree" title="Evolutionary tree">evolutionary tree</a> for the species examined. However, due to the slow mutation rates it experiences, it is often hard to distinguish between closely related species to any large degree, so other methods of analysis must be used.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_mitochondrial_bottleneck">The mitochondrial bottleneck</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mitochondrial_DNA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: The mitochondrial bottleneck">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Entities undergoing uniparental inheritance and with little to no recombination may be expected to be subject to <a href="/wiki/Muller%27s_ratchet" title="Muller's ratchet">Muller's ratchet</a>, the accumulation of deleterious mutations until functionality is lost. Animal populations of mitochondria avoid this buildup through a developmental process known as the <a href="/wiki/Heteroplasmy#Mitochondrial_bottleneck" title="Heteroplasmy">mtDNA bottleneck</a>. The bottleneck exploits <a href="/wiki/Cellular_noise" title="Cellular noise">stochastic processes in the cell</a> to increase in the cell-to-cell variability in <a href="/wiki/Heteroplasmy" title="Heteroplasmy">mutant load</a> as an organism develops: a single egg cell with some proportion of mutant mtDNA thus produces an embryo where different cells have different mutant loads. Cell-level selection may then act to remove those cells with more mutant mtDNA, leading to a stabilisation or reduction in mutant load between generations. The mechanism underlying the bottleneck is debated,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> with a recent mathematical and experimental metastudy providing evidence for a combination of random partitioning of mtDNAs at cell divisions and random turnover of mtDNA molecules within the cell.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid26035426_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid26035426-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Male_inheritance">Male inheritance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mitochondrial_DNA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Male inheritance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Paternal_mtDNA_transmission" title="Paternal mtDNA transmission">Paternal mtDNA transmission</a></div>
<p>Doubly uniparental inheritance of mtDNA is observed in bivalve mollusks. In those species, females have only one type of mtDNA (F), whereas males have F type mtDNA in their somatic cells, but M type of mtDNA (which can be as much as 30% divergent) in <a href="/wiki/Germline" title="Germline">germline</a> cells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> Paternally inherited mitochondria have additionally been reported in some insects such as <a href="/wiki/Drosophila" title="Drosophila">fruit flies</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Honeybee" title="Honeybee">honeybees</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magicicada" title="Magicicada">periodical cicadas</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<p>Male mitochondrial inheritance was recently discovered in <a href="/wiki/Plymouth_Rock_chicken" title="Plymouth Rock chicken">Plymouth Rock chickens</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> Evidence supports rare instances of male mitochondrial inheritance in some mammals as well. Specifically, documented occurrences exist for mice,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> where the male-inherited mitochondria were subsequently rejected. It has also been found in sheep,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> and in cloned cattle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> It has been found in a single case in a human male.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<p>Although many of these cases involve cloned embryos or subsequent rejection of the paternal mitochondria, others document <i><a href="/wiki/In_vivo" title="In vivo">in vivo</a></i> inheritance and persistence under lab conditions.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mitochondrial_donation">Mitochondrial donation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mitochondrial_DNA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Mitochondrial donation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mitochondrial_donation" title="Mitochondrial donation">Mitochondrial donation</a></div>
<p>An IVF technique known as mitochondrial donation or mitochondrial replacement therapy (MRT) results in offspring containing mtDNA from a donor female, and nuclear DNA from the mother and father. In the spindle transfer procedure, the nucleus of an egg is inserted into the cytoplasm of an egg from a donor female which has had its nucleus removed, but still contains the donor female's mtDNA. The composite egg is then fertilized with the male's sperm. The procedure is used when a woman with <a href="/wiki/Mitochondrial_disease" title="Mitochondrial disease">genetically defective mitochondria</a> wishes to procreate and produce offspring with healthy mitochondria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Frith_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Frith-36">[36]</a></sup> The first known child to be born as a result of mitochondrial donation was a boy born to a Jordanian couple in Mexico on 6 April 2016.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Structure">Structure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mitochondrial_DNA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Structure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Circular_versus_linear">Circular versus linear</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mitochondrial_DNA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Circular versus linear">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In most multicellular organisms, the mtDNA – or mitogenome – is organized as a circular, <a href="/wiki/Covalent_bond" title="Covalent bond">covalently</a> closed, double-stranded <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a>. But in many unicellular (e.g. the <a href="/wiki/Ciliate" title="Ciliate">ciliate</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Tetrahymena" title="Tetrahymena">Tetrahymena</a></i> or the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Green_alga" title="Green alga">green alga</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Chlamydomonas_reinhardtii" title="Chlamydomonas reinhardtii">Chlamydomonas reinhardtii</a></i>) and in rare cases also in multicellular organisms (e.g. in some species of <i><a href="/wiki/Cnidaria" title="Cnidaria">Cnidaria</a></i> ) the mtDNA is found as linearly organized <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a>. Most of these linear mtDNAs possess <a href="/wiki/Telomerase" title="Telomerase">telomerase</a> independent <a href="/wiki/Telomere" title="Telomere">telomeres</a> (i.e. the ends of the linear <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a>) with different modes of replication, which have made them interesting objects of research, as many of these unicellular organisms with linear mtDNA are known <a href="/wiki/Pathogen" title="Pathogen">pathogens</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_mammals">In mammals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mitochondrial_DNA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: In mammals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>For <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_mitochondrial_DNA" title="Human mitochondrial DNA">human mitochondrial DNA</a> (and probably for that of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metazoans" title="Metazoans">metazoans</a> in general), 100–10,000 separate copies of mtDNA are usually present per <a href="/wiki/Somatic_cell" title="Somatic cell">somatic cell</a> (egg and <a href="/wiki/Spermatozoon" title="Spermatozoon">sperm cells</a> are exceptions). In mammals, each double-stranded circular mtDNA <a href="/wiki/Molecule" title="Molecule">molecule</a> consists of 15,000–17,000<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Base_pair" title="Base pair">base pairs</a>. The two strands of mtDNA are differentiated by their nucleotide content, with a <a href="/wiki/Guanine" title="Guanine">guanine</a>-rich strand referred to as the <a href="/wiki/Heavy_strand" title="Heavy strand">heavy strand</a> (or H-strand) and a <a href="/wiki/Cytosine" title="Cytosine">cytosine</a>-rich strand referred to as the <a href="/wiki/Heavy_strand" title="Heavy strand">light strand</a> (or L-strand). The heavy strand encodes 28 genes, and the light strand encodes 9 genes for a total of 37 genes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-4">[4]</a></sup> Of the 37 genes, 13 are for proteins (polypeptides), 22 are for <a href="/wiki/Transfer_RNA" title="Transfer RNA">transfer RNA</a> (tRNA) and two are for the small and large subunits of <a href="/wiki/Ribosomal_RNA" title="Ribosomal RNA">ribosomal RNA</a> (rRNA).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NCBI_NC_012920_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-NCBI_NC_012920-40">[40]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_mitogenome" title="Human mitogenome">human mitogenome</a> contains <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Overlapping_genes" title="Overlapping genes">overlapping genes</a> (<a href="/wiki/MT-ATP8" title="MT-ATP8">ATP8</a> and <a href="/wiki/MT-ATP6" title="MT-ATP6">ATP6</a> as well as <a href="/wiki/MT-ND4L" title="MT-ND4L">ND4L</a> and <a href="/wiki/MT-ND4" title="MT-ND4">ND4</a>: see the <a href="#Map_of_the_human_mitochondrial_genome">human mitochondrial genome map</a>), a feature that is rare in animal genomes.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The 37-gene pattern is also seen among most metazoans, although in some cases one or more of these genes is absent and the mtDNA size range is greater.</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_plants">In plants</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mitochondrial_DNA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: In plants">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Great variation in mtDNA gene content and size exists among fungi and plants, although there appears to be a core subset of genes that are present in all eukaryotes (except for the few that have no mitochondria at all).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Johnston.2C_I._G._and_Williams.2C_B._P._2016_10-2"><a href="#cite_note-Johnston.2C_I._G._and_Williams.2C_B._P._2016-10">[10]</a></sup> Some plant species have enormous mitochondrial genomes, with <i>Silene conica</i> mtDNA containing as many as 11,300,000 base pairs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> Surprisingly, even those huge mtDNAs contain the same number and kinds of genes as related plants with much smaller mtDNAs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> The genome of the mitochondrion of the cucumber (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cucumis_sativus" title="Cucumis sativus">Cucumis sativus</a></i>) consists of three circular chromosomes (lengths 1556, 84 and 45 kilobases), which are entirely or largely autonomous with regard to their <a href="/wiki/DNA_replication" title="DNA replication">replication</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alverson2011_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Alverson2011-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_protists">In protists</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mitochondrial_DNA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: In protists">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The smallest mitochondrial genome sequenced to date is the 5967 bp mtDNA of the parasite <i><a href="/wiki/Plasmodium_falciparum" title="Plasmodium falciparum">Plasmodium falciparum</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Genome_diversity">Genome diversity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mitochondrial_DNA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Genome diversity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>There are six main genome types found in mitochondrial genomes. These genome types were classified by “Kolesnikov &amp; Gerasimov (2012)" and differ in various ways such as a circular versus linear genome, genome size, the presence of <a href="/wiki/Intron" title="Intron">introns</a> or <a href="/wiki/Plasmid" title="Plasmid">plasmid like structures</a>, and whether the genetic material is a singular molecule or collection of <a href="/wiki/Homogeneity_and_heterogeneity" title="Homogeneity and heterogeneity">homogeneous</a> or <a href="/wiki/Homogeneity_and_heterogeneity" title="Homogeneity and heterogeneity">heterogeneous</a> molecules.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kolesnikov.2C_A._A._2012_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kolesnikov.2C_A._A._2012-45">[45]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Animals">Animals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mitochondrial_DNA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Animals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>There is only one mitochondrial genome type found in animal cells. This genome contains one circular molecule with between 11–28kbp of genetic material (type 1).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kolesnikov.2C_A._A._2012_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kolesnikov.2C_A._A._2012-45">[45]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Plants_and_fungi">Plants and fungi</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mitochondrial_DNA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Plants and fungi">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>There are three different genome types found in plants and fungi. The first type is a circular genome that has introns (type 2) and may range from 19–1000kbp in length. The second genome type is a circular genome (about 20–1000kbp) that also has a plasmid-like structure (1kb) (type 3). The final genome type that can be found in plant and fungi is a linear genome made up of homogeneous DNA molecules (type 5).</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Protists">Protists</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mitochondrial_DNA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Protists">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Protists contain the most diverse mitochondrial genomes, with five different types found in this kingdom. Type 2, type 3 and type 5 mentioned in the plant and fungal genomes also exists in some protist, as well as two unique genome types. The first of these is a heterogeneous collection of circular DNA molecules (type 4) and the final genome type found in protists is a heterogeneous collection of linear molecules (type 6). Genome types 4 and 6 both range from 1–200kbp in size.</p>
<p>Endosymbiotic gene transfer, the process of genes that were coded in the mitochondrial genome being transferred to the cell's main genome likely explains why more complex organisms, such as humans, have smaller mitochondrial genomes than simpler organisms, such as protists.</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Replication">Replication</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mitochondrial_DNA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Replication">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Mitochondrial DNA is replicated by the <a href="/wiki/DNA_polymerase" title="DNA polymerase">DNA polymerase</a> gamma complex which is composed of a 140 kDa catalytic DNA polymerase encoded by the <i><a href="/wiki/POLG" title="POLG">POLG</a></i> gene and two 55 kDa accessory subunits encoded by the <i><a href="/wiki/POLG2" title="POLG2">POLG2</a></i> gene.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> The replisome machinery is formed by DNA polymerase, <a href="/wiki/PEO1" title="PEO1">TWINKLE</a> and mitochondrial <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SSB_protein" title="SSB protein">SSB proteins</a>. <a href="/wiki/PEO1" title="PEO1">TWINKLE</a> is a <a href="/wiki/Helicase" title="Helicase">helicase</a>, which unwinds short stretches of dsDNA in the 5′ to 3′ direction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jemt2011_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jemt2011-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<p>During <a href="/wiki/Embryogenesis" title="Embryogenesis">embryogenesis</a>, replication of mtDNA is strictly down-regulated from the fertilized oocyte through the preimplantation embryo.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-John2010_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-John2010-48">[48]</a></sup> The resulting reduction in per-cell copy number of mtDNA plays a role in the mitochondrial bottleneck, exploiting <a href="/wiki/Cellular_noise" title="Cellular noise">cell-to-cell variability</a> to ameliorate the inheritance of damaging mutations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid26035426_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-pmid26035426-24">[24]</a></sup> At the <a href="/wiki/Blastocyst" title="Blastocyst">blastocyst</a> stage, the onset of mtDNA replication is specific to the cells of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trophectoderm" title="Trophectoderm">trophectoderm</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-John2010_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-John2010-48">[48]</a></sup> In contrast, the cells of the <a href="/wiki/Inner_cell_mass" title="Inner cell mass">inner cell mass</a> restrict mtDNA replication until they receive the <a href="/wiki/Cell_signaling" title="Cell signaling">signals</a> to <a href="/wiki/Cellular_differentiation" title="Cellular differentiation">differentiate</a> to specific cell types.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-John2010_48-2"><a href="#cite_note-John2010-48">[48]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Transcription">Transcription</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mitochondrial_DNA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Transcription">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In animal mitochondria, each DNA strand is transcribed continuously and produces a <a href="/wiki/Messenger_RNA#Monocistronic_versus_polycistronic_mRNA" title="Messenger RNA">polycistronic</a> RNA molecule. Between most (but not all) protein-coding regions, tRNAs are present (see the <a href="#Map_of_the_human_mitochondrial_genome">human mitochondrial genome map</a>). During transcription, the tRNAs acquire their characteristic L-shape that gets recognized and cleaved by specific enzymes. With the mitochondrial RNA processing, individual mRNA, rRNA, and tRNA sequences are released from the primary transcript.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> Folded tRNAs therefore act as secondary structure punctuations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Mutations_and_disease">Mutations and disease</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mitochondrial_DNA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Mutations and disease">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mitochondrial_DNA_en.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1560" data-file-width="1930" height="242" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Mitochondrial_DNA_en.svg/300px-Mitochondrial_DNA_en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Mitochondrial_DNA_en.svg/450px-Mitochondrial_DNA_en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Mitochondrial_DNA_en.svg/600px-Mitochondrial_DNA_en.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a>

<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mitochondrial_DNA_and_diseases.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1401" data-file-width="1734" height="242" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Mitochondrial_DNA_and_diseases.svg/300px-Mitochondrial_DNA_and_diseases.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Mitochondrial_DNA_and_diseases.svg/450px-Mitochondrial_DNA_and_diseases.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Mitochondrial_DNA_and_diseases.svg/600px-Mitochondrial_DNA_and_diseases.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Susceptibility">Susceptibility</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mitochondrial_DNA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Susceptibility">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The concept that mtDNA is particularly susceptible to <a href="/wiki/Reactive_oxygen_species" title="Reactive oxygen species">reactive oxygen species</a> generated by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Respiratory_chain" title="Respiratory chain">respiratory chain</a> due to its proximity remains controversial.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid19796285_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid19796285-51">[51]</a></sup> mtDNA does not accumulate any more oxidative base damage than nuclear DNA.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> It has been reported that at least some types of oxidative DNA damage are repaired more efficiently in mitochondria than they are in the nucleus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> mtDNA is packaged with proteins which appear to be as protective as proteins of the nuclear chromatin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> Moreover, mitochondria evolved a unique mechanism which maintains mtDNA integrity through degradation of excessively damaged genomes followed by replication of intact/repaired mtDNA. This mechanism is not present in the nucleus and is enabled by multiple copies of mtDNA present in mitochondria <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> The outcome of mutation in mtDNA may be an alteration in the coding instructions for some proteins,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> which may have an effect on organism metabolism and/or fitness.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Genetic_illness">Genetic illness</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mitochondrial_DNA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Genetic illness">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Mitochondrial_disease" title="Mitochondrial disease">Mitochondrial disease</a></div>
<p>Mutations of mitochondrial DNA can lead to a number of illnesses including <a href="/wiki/Exercise_intolerance" title="Exercise intolerance">exercise intolerance</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kearns%E2%80%93Sayre_syndrome" title="Kearns–Sayre syndrome">Kearns–Sayre syndrome</a> (KSS), which causes a person to lose full function of heart, eye, and muscle movements. Some evidence suggests that they might be major contributors to the aging process and <a href="/wiki/Aging-associated_diseases" title="Aging-associated diseases">age-associated pathologies</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> Particularly in the context of disease, the proportion of mutant mtDNA molecules in a cell is termed <a href="/wiki/Heteroplasmy" title="Heteroplasmy">heteroplasmy</a>. The within-cell and between-cell distributions of heteroplasmy dictate the onset and severity of disease <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> and are influenced by complicated <a href="/wiki/Cellular_noise" title="Cellular noise">stochastic processes</a> within the cell and during development.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid26035426_24-2"><a href="#cite_note-pmid26035426-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup></p>
<p>Mutations in mitochondrial tRNAs can be responsible for severe diseases like the <a href="/wiki/MELAS_syndrome" title="MELAS syndrome">MELAS</a> and MERRF syndromes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nature_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-nature-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
<p>Mutations in nuclear genes that encode proteins that mitochondria use can also contribute to mitochondrial diseases. These diseases do not follow mitochondrial inheritance patterns, but instead follow Mendelian inheritance patterns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Use_in_disease_diagnosis">Use in disease diagnosis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mitochondrial_DNA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Use in disease diagnosis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Recently a mutation in mtDNA has been used to help diagnose prostate cancer in patients with negative <a href="/wiki/Prostate_biopsy" title="Prostate biopsy">prostate biopsy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid20944788_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid20944788-62">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid20084081_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid20084081-63">[63]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Relationship_with_aging">Relationship with aging</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mitochondrial_DNA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Relationship with aging">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Though the idea is controversial, some evidence suggests a link between aging and mitochondrial genome dysfunction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> In essence, mutations in mtDNA upset a careful balance of <a href="/wiki/Reactive_oxygen_species" title="Reactive oxygen species">reactive oxygen species</a> (ROS) production and enzymatic ROS scavenging (by enzymes like <a href="/wiki/Superoxide_dismutase" title="Superoxide dismutase">superoxide dismutase</a>, <a href="/wiki/Catalase" title="Catalase">catalase</a>, <a href="/wiki/Glutathione_peroxidase" title="Glutathione peroxidase">glutathione peroxidase</a> and others). However, some mutations that increase ROS production (e.g., by reducing antioxidant defenses) in worms increase, rather than decrease, their longevity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid19796285_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-pmid19796285-51">[51]</a></sup> Also, <a href="/wiki/Naked_mole-rat" title="Naked mole-rat">naked mole rats</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rodent" title="Rodent">rodents</a> about the size of <a href="/wiki/Mouse" title="Mouse">mice</a>, live about eight times longer than mice despite having reduced, compared to mice, antioxidant defenses and increased oxidative damage to biomolecules.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> Once, there was thought to be a positive feedback loop at work (a 'Vicious Cycle'); as mitochondrial DNA accumulates genetic damage caused by free radicals, the mitochondria lose function and leak free radicals into the cytosol. A decrease in mitochondrial function reduces overall metabolic efficiency.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> However, this concept was conclusively disproved when it was demonstrated that mice, which were genetically altered to accumulate mtDNA mutations at accelerated rate do age prematurely, but their tissues do not produce more ROS as predicted by the 'Vicious Cycle' hypothesis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> Supporting a link between longevity and mitochondrial DNA, some studies have found correlations between biochemical properties of the mitochondrial DNA and the longevity of species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> Extensive research is being conducted to further investigate this link and methods to combat aging. Presently, <a href="/wiki/Gene_therapy" title="Gene therapy">gene therapy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nutraceutical" title="Nutraceutical">nutraceutical</a> supplementation are popular areas of ongoing research.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> Bjelakovic et al. analyzed the results of 78 studies between 1977 and 2012, involving a total of 296,707 participants, and concluded that antioxidant supplements do not reduce all-cause mortality nor extend lifespan, while some of them, such as beta carotene, vitamin E, and higher doses of vitamin A, may actually increase mortality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Correlation_of_the_mtDNA_base_composition_with_animals_lifespan">Correlation of the mtDNA base composition with animals lifespan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mitochondrial_DNA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Correlation of the mtDNA base composition with animals lifespan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Correlation_between_the_mtDNA_GC%25_and_maximum_life_span_across_387_different_mammalian_species.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="837" data-file-width="1682" height="109" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Correlation_between_the_mtDNA_GC%25_and_maximum_life_span_across_387_different_mammalian_species.png/220px-Correlation_between_the_mtDNA_GC%25_and_maximum_life_span_across_387_different_mammalian_species.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Correlation_between_the_mtDNA_GC%25_and_maximum_life_span_across_387_different_mammalian_species.png/330px-Correlation_between_the_mtDNA_GC%25_and_maximum_life_span_across_387_different_mammalian_species.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Correlation_between_the_mtDNA_GC%25_and_maximum_life_span_across_387_different_mammalian_species.png/440px-Correlation_between_the_mtDNA_GC%25_and_maximum_life_span_across_387_different_mammalian_species.png 2x" width="220"/></a>

</div>
</div>
<p>Over the past decade, an Israeli research group led by Professor Vadim Fraifeld has shown that extraordinarily strong and significant <a href="/wiki/Correlation_and_dependence" title="Correlation and dependence">correlations</a> exist between the mtDNA base composition and animal species-specific maximum life spans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehmann_et_al..2C_2006_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lehmann_et_al..2C_2006-72">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehmann_et_al..2C_2008_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lehmann_et_al..2C_2008-73">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehmann_et_al..2C_2013_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lehmann_et_al..2C_2013-74">[74]</a></sup> As demonstrated in their work, higher mtDNA <a href="/wiki/Guanine" title="Guanine">guanine</a> + <a href="/wiki/Cytosine" title="Cytosine">cytosine</a> content (<a href="/wiki/GC-content" title="GC-content">GC%</a>) strongly associates with longer <a href="/wiki/Maximum_life_span" title="Maximum life span">maximum life spans</a> across animal species. An additional astonishing observation is that the mtDNA GC% correlation with the maximum life spans is independent of the well-known correlation between animal species metabolic rate and maximum life spans. The mtDNA GC% and resting metabolic rate explain the differences in animal species maximum life spans in a multiplicative manner (i.e., species maximum life span = their mtDNA GC% * metabolic rate).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehmann_et_al..2C_2008_73-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lehmann_et_al..2C_2008-73">[73]</a></sup> To support the scientific community in carrying out comparative analyses between mtDNA features and longevity across animals, a dedicated database was built named <a class="external text" href="http://www.mitoage.info/" rel="nofollow">MitoAge</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span id="Relationship_with_non-B_(non-canonical)_DNA_structures"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Relationship_with_non-B_.28non-canonical.29_DNA_structures">Relationship with non-B (non-canonical) DNA structures</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mitochondrial_DNA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Relationship with non-B (non-canonical) DNA structures">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Deletion breakpoints frequently occur within or near regions showing non-canonical (non-B) conformations, namely hairpins, cruciforms and cloverleaf-like elements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> Moreover, there is data supporting the involvement of helix-distorting intrinsically curved regions and long G-tetrads in eliciting instability events. In addition, higher breakpoint densities were consistently observed within GC-skewed regions and in the close vicinity of the degenerate sequence motif YMMYMNNMMHM.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Use_in_identification">Use in identification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mitochondrial_DNA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Use in identification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For use in human identification, see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_mitochondrial_DNA" title="Human mitochondrial DNA">Human mitochondrial DNA</a>.</div>
<p>Unlike nuclear DNA, which is inherited from both parents and in which genes are rearranged in the process of <a href="/wiki/Genetic_recombination" title="Genetic recombination">recombination</a>, there is usually no change in mtDNA from parent to offspring. Although mtDNA also recombines, it does so with copies of itself within the same mitochondrion. Because of this and because the <a href="/wiki/Mutation_rate" title="Mutation rate">mutation rate</a> of animal mtDNA is higher than that of nuclear DNA,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-78">[78]</a></sup> mtDNA is a powerful tool for tracking ancestry through females (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Matrilineage" title="Matrilineage">matrilineage</a>) and has been used in this role to track the ancestry of many species back hundreds of generations.</p>
<p>The rapid mutation rate (in animals) makes mtDNA useful for assessing genetic relationships of individuals or groups within a species and also for identifying and quantifying the phylogeny (evolutionary relationships; see <a href="/wiki/Phylogenetics" title="Phylogenetics">phylogenetics</a>) among different species. To do this, biologists determine and then compare the mtDNA sequences from different individuals or species. Data from the comparisons is used to construct a network of relationships among the sequences, which provides an estimate of the relationships among the individuals or species from which the mtDNAs were taken. mtDNA can be used to estimate the relationship between both closely related and distantly related species. Due to the high mutation rate of mtDNA in animals, the 3rd positions of the codons change relatively rapidly, and thus provide information about the genetic distances among closely related individuals or species. On the other hand, the substitution rate of mt-proteins is very low, thus amino acid changes accumulate slowly (with corresponding slow changes at 1st and 2nd codon positions) and thus they provide information about the genetic distances of distantly related species. Statistical models that treat substitution rates among codon positions separately, can thus be used to simultaneously estimate phylogenies that contain both closely and distantly related species<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nature_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-nature-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
<p>Mitochondrial DNA was admitted into evidence for the first time ever in 1996 during <i>State of Tennessee v. Paul Ware</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the 1998 court case of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania v. Patricia Lynne Rorrer,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> mitochondrial DNA was admitted into evidence in the State of Pennsylvania for the first time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> The case was featured in episode 55 of season 5 of the true crime drama series <a href="/wiki/Forensic_Files_(season_5)" title="Forensic Files (season 5)">Forensic Files (season 5)</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Mitochondrial DNA was first admitted into evidence in <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a> in the successful prosecution of David Westerfield for the 2002 kidnapping and murder of 7-year-old <a href="/wiki/Murder_of_Danielle_van_Dam" title="Murder of Danielle van Dam">Danielle van Dam</a> in <a href="/wiki/San_Diego" title="San Diego">San Diego</a>: it was used for both human and dog identification.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> This was the first trial in the U.S. to admit canine DNA.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup></p>
<p>The remains of <a href="/wiki/Richard_III_of_England" title="Richard III of England">King Richard III</a> were identified by comparing his mtDNA with that of two matrilineal descendants of his sister.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mitochondrial_DNA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Mitochondrial DNA was discovered in the 1960s by Margit M. K. Nass and Sylvan Nass by <a href="/wiki/Electron_microscope" title="Electron microscope">electron microscopy</a> as DNase-sensitive threads inside mitochondria,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> and by Ellen Haslbrunner, <a href="/wiki/Hans_Tuppy" title="Hans Tuppy">Hans Tuppy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gottfried_Schatz" title="Gottfried Schatz">Gottfried Schatz</a> by biochemical assays on highly purified mitochondrial fractions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Mitochondrial_sequence_databases">Mitochondrial sequence databases</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mitochondrial_DNA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Mitochondrial sequence databases">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Several specialized databases have been founded to collect mitochondrial genome sequences and other information. Although most of them focus on sequence data, some of them include phylogenetic or functional information.</p>
<ul>
<li><b>MitoSatPlant</b>: Mitochondrial microsatellites database of viridiplantae.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MitoSatPlant_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-MitoSatPlant-88">[88]</a></sup></li>
<li><b>MitoBreak</b>: the mitochondrial DNA breakpoints database.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MitoBreak_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-MitoBreak-89">[89]</a></sup></li>
<li><b>MitoFish</b> and <b>MitoAnnotator</b>: a mitochondrial genome database of fish.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MitoFish_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-MitoFish-90">[90]</a></sup> See also Cawthorn et al.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cawthorn_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cawthorn-91">[91]</a></sup></li>
<li><b>MitoZoa</b> 2.0: a database for comparative and evolutionary analyses of mitochondrial genomes in Metazoa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MitoZoa_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-MitoZoa-92">[92]</a></sup> (no longer available)</li>
<li><b>InterMitoBase</b>: an annotated database and analysis platform of protein-protein interactions for human mitochondria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-InterMitoBase_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-InterMitoBase-93">[93]</a></sup> (apparently last updated in 2010, but still available)</li>
<li><b>Mitome:</b> a database for comparative mitochondrial genomics in metazoan animals<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mitome_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mitome-94">[94]</a></sup> (no longer available)</li>
<li><b>MitoRes:</b> a resource of nuclear-encoded mitochondrial genes and their products in metazoa<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MitoRes_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-MitoRes-95">[95]</a></sup> (apparently no longer being updated)</li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Mitochondrial_mutation_databases">Mitochondrial mutation databases</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mitochondrial_DNA&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Mitochondrial mutation databases">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Several specialized databases exist that report polymorphisms and mutations in the human mitochondrial DNA, together with the assessment of their pathogenicity.</p>
<ul>
<li><b>MITOMAP</b>: A compendium of polymorphisms and mutations in human mitochondrial DNA <a class="external autonumber" href="http://www.mitomap.org/MITOMAP" rel="nofollow">[3]</a>.</li>
<li><b>MitImpact</b>: A collection of pre-computed pathogenicity predictions for all nucleotide changes that cause non-synonymous substitutions in human mitochondrial protein coding genes <a class="external autonumber" href="http://mitimpact.css-mendel.it/" rel="nofollow">[4]</a>.</li>
</ul>
